<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="55e3dca75afd403a9a58caf18d2f3c28"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="55e3dca75afd403a9a58caf18d2f3c28">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd6718440-a606-4225-9ecb-72176f4eced0'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Zhang/Qingrui">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Peng/Qiuming" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Qiuming Peng<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Qiuming Peng</p>
                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">
                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a class="sm-account__link" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:pengqiuming@ysu.edu.cn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-ic_mail_solid"></i><span>pengqiuming@ysu.edu.cn</span></a></li>

                     <p>State Key Laboratory of Metastable Materials Science and Technology, Yanshan University, Qinhuangdao, 066004 P. R. China</p>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d196546113" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium (Na) batteries are being considered as prospective candidates for the next generation of secondary batteries in contrast to lithium-based batteries, due to their high raw-material abundance, low cost, and sustainability. However, the unfavorable growth of Na-metal deposition and severe interfacial reactions have prevented their large-scale applications. Here, a vacuum filtration strategy, through amyloid-fibril-modified glass-fiber separators, is proposed to address these issues. The modified symmetric cell can be cycled for 1800&nbsp;h, surpassing the performance of previously reported Na-based electrodes under an ester-based electrolyte. Moreover, the Na/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell with a sodiophilic amyloid-fibril-modified separator exhibits a capacity retention of 87.13% even after 1000 cycles. Both the experimental and the theoretical results show that the sodiophilic amyloid fibril homogenizes the electric field and Na-ion concentration, fundamentally inhibiting dendrite formation. Simultaneously, the glutamine amino acids in the amyloid fibril have the highest adsorption energy for Na, resulting in the formation of a stable Na<sub>3</sub>N- and NaN<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub><i>y</i></sub>-rich solid-electrolyte-interface film on the anode during cycling. This work provides not only a possible pathway to solve the dendrite problem in metal batteries using environmentally friendly biomacromolecular materials, but also a new direction for expanding biomaterial applications.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202304942-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202304942-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The dramatic growth of high-power electronic devices, coupled with the fact that traditional Li ion batteries are nearly approaching their theoretical energy density limitation, has prompted enthusiasm for more advanced batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0001_R_d196546101e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Renewable Sustainable Energy Rev.">1</a>, <a href="#adma202304942-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0002_R_d196546101e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Batteries">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Undoubtedly, the direct application of alkali metals as anodes is a promising strategy for increasing the theoretical specific capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0003_R_d196546101e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Mater. Chem. A">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Theortically, the natural abundance of Na in the earth crust (23.6&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;ppm) is three orders of magnitude greater than that of Li (20&nbsp;ppm).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0004_R_d196546101e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - J. Mater. Chem. A">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the uncontrolled growth of Na dendrites in the presence of commercial ester-based electrolytes results in low Coulombic efficiency (CE), inferior cycling performance and even safety concerns after long-term cell operation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0005_R_d196546101e484" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Trends Chem">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To accelerate the commercialization of rechargeable Na-metal batteries, it is crucial to address the serious issues caused by dendrite growth. Basically, dendrite inhibition can be achieved through modifications of three battery components: the anode, the electrolyte and the separator. Among them, anodic modification can be used to build 3D&nbsp;host structures or to apply sodiophilic coatings to inhibit dendrites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0006_R_d196546101e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a>, <a href="#adma202304942-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0007_R_d196546101e491" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Small">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, anodic modification frequently raises significant safety concerns due to the high activity of Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0008_R_d196546101e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Energy Environ. Sci.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, electrolyte modification can be achieved by adding additives,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0009_R_d196546101e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Asian J.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> although solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film formed using electrolyte additives are relatively stable but does not completely prevent dendrite penetration, especially after long-term cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0010_R_d196546101e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - InfoMat">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Noticeably, the separator is an essential component of the battery and directly affects safety performance. Therefore, the modification of separators to prevent dendrite penetration is an important and promising strategy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0011_R_d196546101e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, it is well known that uneven electric field distributions and ion concentration distributions inevitably form within the pores in the separator during the deposition of Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0012_R_d196546101e518" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Commun.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, both Na ions and electric fields tend to focus at protrusions with high surface energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0013_R_d196546101e523" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, Na nucleation and growth tend to occur more commonly at tip sites, leading to non-uniform Na deposition. The formation of Na protrusions further enhances the strength of the local electric field surrounding them, causing Na protrusions to evolve into Na dendrites during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0014_R_d196546101e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Carbon Energy">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This phenomenon is more serious in ester-based electrolytes due to the difficulty forming stable SEI films on the anode surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0015_R_d196546101e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Commun.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, physical modification based on the separator perspective is better than anode modification to improve the electric field distribution and stabilize Na batteries.</p>
         
         <p>Biomacromolecular structures such as proteins are enriched with polar groups can homogenize ionic fluxes and induce uniform metal deposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0016_R_d196546101e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, modified soybean protein fibers provide a good modulation of Li deposition by forming a LiF-rich SEI membrane.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0017_R_d196546101e547" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Lett.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, collagen hydrolysate induces a deionization shock and diffuses cations on Li or Zn anode to promote homogeneous metal deposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0018_R_d196546101e552" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Sci. Adv.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, there is still a lack of interpretation on how protein molecules affect metal deposition and SEI components, and in particular which amino acids play an crucial impact during repeated cycles.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we propose that filtering a uniform layer of amyloid fibrils (AF) over the separator physically induces a uniform electric field and Na<sup>+</sup> concentration, which fundamentally inhibits the formation of dendrites, and additionally generates a stable SEI film further stabilizing the Na-metal anode. Specifically, we found that the addition of AF allowed an increased proportion of Na-dense crystalline surfaces to occur during Na deposition, which enhanced the reversibility of the Na-metal battery. We also discover that glutamine amino acids in amyloid fibrils have the highest binding energy for Na, resulting in the presence of a Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaN<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub><i>y</i></sub> rich SEI film during cycling, and then it improves the interface generated by the ester-based electrolyte on the anode surface. Therefore, a unique dendrite-free metal deposition has been achieved in terms of physical fiber structure and chemical composition modulation in the amyloid fibrils. In this case, the symmetrical Na-metal batteries remain stable cycle over 1800&nbsp;h, which is nearly 6 times higher than that of unmodified battery. Moreover, the capacity retention rate of amyloid fibrils modified Na-metal full cells coupled with the high load cathode (Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>) can provide 87.13% high-capacity retention rate even after 1000 cycles, which are far higher than those of the unmodified counterparts (69.46% after 200 cycles).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202304942-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202304942-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202304942-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202304942-sec-0030-title">2.1 Preparation of Amyloid Fibrils@Glass-Fiber Separator</h3>
            
            <p>The amyloid fibrils@glass-fiber (AF@GF) separator was prepared in two steps: the synthesis of amyloid fibrils in combination with the vacuum filtration of amyloid fibrils onto the GF separator (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0001">1a</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Typically, we first prepared amyloid fibrils containing a variety of amino acids through a series of methods such as acid washing, dialysis and hydrolysis based on the abundance of amyloid fibrils in β-lactoglobulin, as described in the materials preparation section. Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectroscopy is used to analyze the AF1, AF5, and AF10 samples, which are produced by hydrolysis for 1, 5, and 10&nbsp;h, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0001">1b</a>). The typical protein absorption peaks at 1649.1 and 1535.3&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are observed, assigning to amide I (C≐O stretching) and amide III (N─H in-plane bending/C─N stretching), respectively. These peaks are mainly associated with the vibrations of peptide bonds in the AF samples.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0019_R_d196546101e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Eng. J.">19</a>, <a href="#adma202304942-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0020_R_d196546101e609" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Biochim. Biophys. Acta, Gen. Subj.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, other absorption peaks at 1450.4&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (C─H<sub>2/3</sub>), 1234.4&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (C─O stretching), 2964.5&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (C─H<sub>3</sub>), 3292.3&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (O─H) and 3411.9&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> (N─H) are also demonstrated.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0021_R_d196546101e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">21</a>, <a href="#adma202304942-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0022_R_d196546101e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The abundance of polar groups in the AF5 is expected to effectively reduce the concentration polarization in the Na-metal battery.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0023_R_d196546101e635" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Energy">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of N in the AF5 is demonstrated by the amide/NH<sub>3</sub><sup>+</sup> at 400.1&nbsp;eV and N─H/C─N at 399.7&nbsp;eV in the N1s spectrum, while the presence of C and O is chiefly in the form of C─C, C≐O, C─O, and O─H (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0019_R_d196546101e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Eng. J.">19</a>, <a href="#adma202304942-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0024_R_d196546101e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Cleaner Prod.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, Raman spectroscopy indicates that the amide I band at 1669.3&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in the AF5 is considered to be the C≐O bond, relative to the absorption of amide I being caused by the stretching vibration of the short C≐O bond in protein (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0001">1d</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0025_R_d196546101e660" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Food Hydrocolloids">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The other characteristic peaks at 1449.7 and 1238.6&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> are assigned to the presence of amide III, in terms of C─N stretching and N─H bending vibrations of the peptide bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0026_R_d196546101e668" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Agric. Food Chem.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202304942-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4f586855-eaa6-4ece-aa90-71695d309f99/adma202304942-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/4f586855-eaa6-4ece-aa90-71695d309f99/adma202304942-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/fbd351b0-805f-4d3f-8096-85704e621aef/adma202304942-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4f586855-eaa6-4ece-aa90-71695d309f99/adma202304942-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304942-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304942" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the synthetic process of the AF5@GF separator. b) FT-IR patterns of AF1, AF5, and AF10. c) XPS profiles of the AF5 in N1s spectrum. d) Raman spectrum of the AF5. e) TEM image of the AF5. f–j) Longitudinal-section SEM image and corresponding mapping of the AF5@GF separator.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Morphology variation of the produced AF was further investigated by transmission electron microscopy (TEM), in which the hydrolysis time remarkably affects morphologies of the AF (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0001">1e</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Specifically, the dimensions of the AF5 (hydrolysis time for 5&nbsp;h) are ≈3.3&nbsp;µm in length and 12.4&nbsp;nm in diameter, but the length and diameter values of the AF1 change to 1.3&nbsp;µm and 42&nbsp;nm, respectively. Worse, the morphology of the AF10 is indistinguishable, probably due to long hydrolysis time leading to amyloid fibrils agglomeration and breakage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0027_R_d196546101e700" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Biomacromolecules">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In this regard, the AF5 may expose more surface groups compared with other samples. After the AF5 has been prepared, it is decorated to the GF separator by vacuum filtration. During the compounding process, the ionic conductivity (<i>σ</i>) of AF5@GF decreases slightly in contrast to that of the GF separator, but the GF does not react with the AF5 to form new substances (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images reveal that the amyloid fibrils deposited on the GF separator exhibits a disordered morphology. Notably, the amyloid fibrils agglomerate on the surface due to fibril gelation (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) when the concentration of the amyloid fibril solution is 5&nbsp;wt%. The SEM and energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) data reveal that the thickness of the deposited amyloid fibril is 1, 5, and 10&nbsp;µm, with the AF5 concentrations of 1, 2, and 5&nbsp;wt%, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0001">1f–j</a>, Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). According to these observations, we selected an amyloid fibril concentration of 2&nbsp;wt% for subsequent testing, which can achieve more uniform and abundant amyloid fibril deposition but avoiding fibril gelation.</p>
            
            <p>To further assess the effects of AF on separator intrinsic properties, we investigated their related physical properties, such as wettability, mechanical properties, elastic modulus and adhesion force. Both separators are found to be wettable by the liquid electrolyte (1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC:DEC&nbsp;=&nbsp;1:1&nbsp;vol% with 5.0&nbsp;wt% FEC), with initial contact angles of 28.4° and 34.8° for the GF separator and the AF5@GF composite separator, respectively. (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Video&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, the stress–strain curves (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information) display the tensile strength of the AF5@GF separator is ≈5.1 times higher than that of the commercial GF separator. Meanwhile, the elastic modulus is improved remarkably. This trend has further confirmed by atomic force microscopy (AFM) via quantitative nano-mechanics mode, wherein the elastic modulus of the AF5@GF separator surface is found to be ≈25.2 ± 0.6&nbsp;MPa, which is higher than that of the GF separator, at 13.9 ± 0.8&nbsp;MPa. Besides, the adhesion force of the AF5@GF separator has enhanced at least 3.5 times higher than that of the GF separator (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). These findings demonstrate that the AF5@GF separator exhibits superior mechanical resistance and excellent puncture resistance, which might effectively prevent Na dendrites from penetrating the separator.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0028_R_d196546101e743" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202304942-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202304942-sec-0040-title">2.2 Sodium Deposition</h3>
            
            <p>To probe Na deposition behavior of separator, we studied the reversibility of Na plating/stripping for the Na|Al asymmetric batteries with the GF separator or the AF5@GF separator in a conventional ester-based electrolyte. TEM and FTIR test results show that there is no significant change in the morphology and functional groups of AF5 after immersion in the electrolyte for 24&nbsp;h, implying that the AF5 is stable in the electrolyte (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0002">2a</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), the CEs of the Na|Al batteries with the GF separator exhibit a slow rise for ≈30 cycles followed by a decline, and the average CEs remain at a low value of 68.8% during the initial 50 cycles, when operated at 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. However, the Na|Al batteries with the AF5@GF separator can run for 150 cycles with an average CE of 91.6%. In addition, the nucleation overpotential of the Na|Al battery assembled with the AF5@GF separator is only 0.245&nbsp;V, which is 9% lower than the bare GF separator, signifying that the deposition barrier of Na ion on the former can be reduced on the electrode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0002">2b</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202304942-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/253a5647-691d-49fc-acde-a66eaa82e206/adma202304942-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/253a5647-691d-49fc-acde-a66eaa82e206/adma202304942-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e0b2c34d-eeb8-4d06-9402-509e6f821dfd/adma202304942-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/253a5647-691d-49fc-acde-a66eaa82e206/adma202304942-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304942-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304942" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Coulombic efficiency of different separator at 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and 1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. b) Voltage profiles of Na nucleation under different separator at 1&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. c) Percentage of (110) and (200) crystalline surfaces of Na deposited under different separator. d,e) In situ XRD patterns of Na deposition processes in Na|Al half-cells with GF separator (d) or AF5@GF separator (e). f,g) Na deposition on Al current collector using GF separator (f) or AF5@GF separator (g). h,i) Na deposition on the GF separator (h) or the AF5@GF separator (i). j–m) Simulate the electric field distribution on Na anode without AF5 (j,k) and with AF5 (l,m) after 500&nbsp;min.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <div class="paragraph-element">The performance of the AF5@GF separator is better than that of the GF separator, which may be associated with the deposition morphology of Na.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0012_R_d196546101e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Commun.">12</a>, <a href="#adma202304942-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0018_R_d196546101e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Sci. Adv.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The difference in Na deposition morphology with and without the AF5 modification has been clarified by in situ XRD, SEM, and COMSOL simulations. On the one hand, in situ XRD patterns show that the proportion of (110) crystal plane deposition of Na after the AF5 modification increased to 77.39%, while the proportion of low index (200) crystal plane decreased to 22.61% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0002">2c–e</a>). The increased proportion of close-packed face of the deposited metal enhances the density of deposition and thus improves the reversibility of the batteries, which is consistent with the phenomenon existing in other metal batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0029_R_d196546101e813" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nat. Commun.">29</a>, <a href="#adma202304942-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0030_R_d196546101e813" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Nano Res.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, SEM images of Al current collector retrieved from the cycled Na|Al batteries display inhomogeneous loose structure with Na dendrites on Al current collectors using the bare GF separators at a deposition capacity of 1&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0002">2f</a>). Such incompact structures may be related to the inhomogeneous nucleation and deposition of Na caused by the uneven distribution of the electric field.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0012_R_d196546101e824" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Commun.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, Na dendrites are further evolved by the non-uniform deposition of Na through a self-amplifying mechanism (<b>Scheme</b> <a href="#adma202304942-fig-0006">1</a>). On the contrary, dendrite-free deposition morphology on the Al collector in the Na|Al battery assembled with the AF5@GF separator is observed (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0002">2g</a>, Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0006">1</a>). Moreover, the corresponding SEM image of the AF5@GF verifies that Na deposition might be achieved on this separator, while the pristine GF separator is still dendritic Na appears (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0002">2h–i</a>). Finally, 2D finite element models are constructed to characterize the ion migration, electric field distribution and dendrite growth near the electrodes. The dendrites with a diameter of 0.5&nbsp;µm are taken to compare the dendrite growth on the electrode with and without amyloid fibrils at the initial state. The governing equations are as follows:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0031_R_d196546101e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Power Sources">31</a>, <a href="#adma202304942-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0032_R_d196546101e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adma202304942-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/98020eef-2811-48f0-9f18-ac9c3a64091d/adma202304942-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202304942:adma202304942-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adma202304942-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>N</mi>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                              <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>D</mi>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mo>∇</mo>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>c</mi>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
                              </msub>

                              <msub>
                                 <mi>z</mi>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>u</mi>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi>
                                    <mo>,</mo>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </msub>
                              <mi>F</mi>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>c</mi>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
                              </msub>

                              <mi>V</mi>

                              <msub>
                                 <mi>c</mi>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>
                              </msub>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation} {N}_{\mathrm{i}}=\ -{D}_{\mathrm{i}}\nabla {c}_{\mathrm{i}}-{z}_{\mathrm{i}}{u}_{\mathrm{m},\mathrm{i}}F{c}_{\mathrm{i}}\nabla V+u{c}_{\mathrm{i}} \end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>N</i><sub>i</sub> is the mass flux for each species, <i>D</i><sub>i</sub> is the diffusion coefficient, <i>c</i><sub>i</sub> is the concentration, <i>z</i><sub>i</sub> is the charge number, <i>V</i> is the electrolyte potential, and <i>F</i> is Faraday constant. Convection is not considered in this process, so <i>u</i> is 0. The model is solved in the solver COMSOL multiphasic. Overall, COMSOL simulations show that severe Na<sup>+</sup> polarization and electric field inhomogeneity occur in the AF5-free modified Na batteries after 500&nbsp;min, leading to free growth of Na dendrites (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0002">2j,k</a>, Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12a–d</a>, Supporting Information). In turn, the local Na<sup>+</sup> concentration of the battery model modified by the AF5 is obviously uniform, and the local electric field strength is ameliorated, which effectively inhibits the dendrite formation caused by mass transport and enhances sodium ion flux. (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0002">2l,m</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12e–h</a>, Supporting Information), in line with the XRD and SEM results. Besides, the calculated Na<sup>+</sup> transference number (tNa<sup>+</sup>) for the AF5@GF is 0.82, which is slightly higher than that of the GF (0.80) (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). The main reason is attributed to the presence of polar –COO<sup>−</sup> groups, which can inhibit the transport of ClO<sub>4</sub><sup>−</sup> in the electrolyte but favor for the passage of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0033_R_d196546101e956" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Solid State Ionics">33</a>, <a href="#adma202304942-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0034_R_d196546101e956" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Molecules">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Simultaneously, the repulsive force among AF makes it easy to form uniform and stable channels during the film formation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0035_R_d196546101e962" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Carbohydr. Polym.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202304942-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/461c21ac-ac01-4e27-aa5d-90d8b2812d73/adma202304942-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/461c21ac-ac01-4e27-aa5d-90d8b2812d73/adma202304942-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b0014406-7805-4d9c-9f00-5dd4e0da8aae/adma202304942-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/461c21ac-ac01-4e27-aa5d-90d8b2812d73/adma202304942-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304942-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304942" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic representation of Na growth in Na cells before and after amyloid fibrils modification.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202304942-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202304942-sec-0050-title">2.3 Sodiophilic Mechanism of Amyloid Fibril Components</h3>
            
            <p>To understand the outstanding electrochemical behavior of AF-modified separator, we appraised the roles of different groups (leucine: C<sub>6</sub>H<sub>13</sub>NO<sub>2</sub>, alanine: C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>7</sub>NO<sub>2</sub>, and glutamine: C<sub>5</sub>H<sub>10</sub>N<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>) on AF surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0036_R_d196546101e1016" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Density functional theory (DFT) calculations are employed to investigate the Na binding energy of various amino acids in amyloid fibrils, with the aim of elucidating their sodiophilic properties. Detailed insight into the initial and stable adsorption configurations of six different Na adsorption sites is performed for each amino acid, as shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0003">3a–c</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14–16</a> (Supporting Information). It is worth noting that the positions with higher electronegativity have a greater propensity for Na atom adsorption, as shown by the configuration changes during our analyses. Additionally, the interaction of Na atom with the surfaces of leucine or alanine is looser than the interaction of Na atom with the surface of glutamine. The possible reason is related to the small binding energies between the two amino acids and Na atoms. In contrast, the latter has a large negative binding energy. Evidently, glutamine site 1 has the highest binding energy of −0.449&nbsp;V for Na atom, indicating that glutamine is the most sodiophilic reason in the amyloid fibril.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0013_R_d196546101e1030" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202304942-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/52feab57-9988-4eae-b270-18e7f341796e/adma202304942-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/52feab57-9988-4eae-b270-18e7f341796e/adma202304942-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a61f30a3-e9dd-486a-8411-3b884c88a237/adma202304942-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/52feab57-9988-4eae-b270-18e7f341796e/adma202304942-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304942-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304942" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–c) Summary of calculated binding energies of Na with leucine, alanine, and glutamine in amyloid fibril. (Inset: Stable structure model after Na adsorption at different sites). The Na, C, N, O, and H atoms are marked as yellow, brown, blue, red, and pink, respectively. d–f) The (100) plane of the three-dimensional charge density differences with isosurface value of 0.01&nbsp;e&nbsp;Bohr<sup>−3</sup> of leucine (d), alanine (e), and glutamine (f) with one Na atom adsorbed. Blue and yellow colors represent losing and gaining electrons, respectively. g–i) The electronic charge density differences contour plots on (100) plane of leucine (g), alanine (h), and glutamine (i) with one Na atom adsorbed.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>To further understand the interaction between amino acids and Na atoms, the charge density difference has been calculated to compare adsorption sites of different amino acids on Na atoms (Site 1). Specifically, the electrons are restricted within the adsorption sites on the leucine and alanine surfaces, weakening the coupling interactions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0003">3d,e</a>). However, the charge transfer from Na to the glutamine site1 is more pronounced, demonstrating the delocalized electron in-between the conjugation structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0003">3f</a>). In addition, the strong adsorption strength of site 1 can also be elucidated in terms of the 2D electronic structure. According to the (100) crystal plane projection, the glutamine exhibits the highest charge density difference around Na in contrast to leucine and alanine. Correspondingly, the projection on the (010) and (001) planes exhibits a similar situation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0003">3g–i</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the glutamine in the amyloid fibril has strongest affinity with Na atom.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0006_R_d196546101e1072" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Energy Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202304942-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202304942-sec-0060-title">2.4 Battery Performance</h3>
            
            <p>To evaluate the cycling stability of the AF5@GF separator, the symmetric Na||Na batteries were fabricated. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0004">4a</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18a,b</a> (Supporting Information) exhibit the voltage-time curves of Na||Na batteries with different separators at 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. Notably, the batteries with the AF5@GF separator deliver virtually flat voltage plateaus over 1800&nbsp;h with small overpotential (120&nbsp;mV), illustrating their good interfacial stability and cyclability. However, for the batteries with the GF separators, the voltage hysteresis seriously plummets after 310&nbsp;h, indicating that the battery has appeared short circuit. In addition, the AF has a marginal effect on the overpotential, probably due to the nature of the biomolecule itself, which is common in protein-modified metal cells.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0037_R_d196546101e1097" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">37</a>, <a href="#adma202304942-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0040_R_d196546101e1097" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Nat. Commun.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Inspiringly, we have achieved significant improvements in stabilizing cycle life of Na-metal batteries by means of AF modified separators in ester-based electrolytes, compared to sodiophilic coating techniques (Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, Mg-MOF-74, graphene derivatives, NaBr/Na<sub>3</sub>P nanocrystallines),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0041_R_d196546101e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a>-<a href="#adma202304942-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0047_R_d196546101e1109" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Nano Lett.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> 3D Na anode designs (carbonized wood composite),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0048_R_d196546101e1115" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Nano Lett.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> electrolyte modification methods (C<sub>60</sub>(NO<sub>2</sub>)<sub>6</sub> additive),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0049_R_d196546101e1127" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nano Energy">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Na alloy modification strategies (Na–Sn alloy),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0050_R_d196546101e1132" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Nano Energy">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as summarized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0004">4b</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18c–h</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). Additionally, the batteries with the GF, AF1@GF, and AF10@GF separators hardly sustain a current density at 5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, whereas the batteries with the AF5@GF separator survives in the current density ranging from 0.5 to 5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>, which can recover to the initial state once the current density returns (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0004">4c</a>). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was also tested after cycling of symmetric cells with the bare GF and the AF5@GF separators at a current density of 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and a capacity of 1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0004">4d,e</a>, Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). For symmetric batteries with the GF separator, continuous dendrite growth is detected because of the uneven distribution of Na ions in the battery system, and the accumulated SEI layer with poor Na-ion conductivity leads to high resistance. By comparison, the symmetric batteries with the AF5@GF separator shows a rather stable and consistently low resistance even after 50 cycles, with only one tenth of the resistance of batteries equipped with the GF separator. It indicates the stable interface properties of the AF5@GF during cycling due to its interconnected matrix.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202304942-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/46cddb70-69d6-4b0c-be8f-93552642599b/adma202304942-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/46cddb70-69d6-4b0c-be8f-93552642599b/adma202304942-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5acf3d63-92a6-4214-883a-2594b57895a2/adma202304942-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/46cddb70-69d6-4b0c-be8f-93552642599b/adma202304942-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304942-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304942" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Voltage–time profiles of Na plating/stripping process at 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> with 1.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. b) Comparison of cycle times of sodium batteries equipped with AF5@GF separator with other reported Na&nbsp;batteries at different current densities in ester-based electrolyte. c) Rate performances of symmetric batteries using different separators at various current density from 0.5 to 5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> with a stripping/plating capacity of 1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. d,e) EIS values of Na symmetric batteries with GF separators (d) or AF5@GF separators (e) after different cycles. The inset is the EIS fitting circuit diagram. f,g) Na surface SEM morphology of Na symmetric battery after 50 cycles with GF separator (f) and 375 cycles with AF5@GF separator (g). h) The N1s spectrum of the Na surface after 0, 1, and 50 cycles of batteries equipped with AF5@GF separators.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The morphology and composition of the Na surface were investigated using SEM images and XPS tests after disassembling symmetric batteries cycled at a current density of 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> and a capacity of 1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>. As cycled number increased, the anode surface in batteries with the AF1@GF and AF10@GF separators exhibits increasingly inhomogeneous, and dendrites are even observed after 125 and 175 cycles, respectively. Even worse, the dendrites present on the anode with the GF separators after only 10 cycles, and the dendrites growth becomes more severe in the following cycles. (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0004">4f</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a–i</a>, Supporting Information). Attractively, a smooth and dense surface can be still observed on the Na anode of the Na-metal battery using the AF5@GF separator, even after 375 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0004">4g</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20j–l</a>, Supporting Information). This further proves that the AF5 can suppress dendrite growth by homogenizing the Na ion concentration, thereby improving the battery cycle stability. Additionally, XPS results suggest that N element in the AF5 is reduced by Na, relative to the formation of Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaN<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub><i>y</i></sub> compounds (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0004">4h</a>). The mixed ion–electron mixed conductivity of the reduced products facilitates ion transport, resulting in a uniform ion concentration at the anode-electrolyte interface, which ultimately improves plating/stripping stability of symmetric batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0051_R_d196546101e1228" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is thought that Na<sub>3</sub>N and NaN<sub><i>x</i></sub>O<sub><i>y</i></sub> are presented during the cycling process because both of them are hardly detected by XPS before the battery is cycled (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0004">4h</a>), consistent with the results obtained from FTIR spectroscopy. This also proves that the AF5 remains stable in the electrolyte prior to cycling and the intensity of the N-containing group peaks of the AF5 decreases relatively after cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a>, Supporting Information). Conversely, the SEI film constituents, including Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>O, and NaF, are present on the Na surface even before the battery is cycled, suggesting that these constituents are spontaneously formed via the reaction between Na and the electrolyte (Scheme&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0006">1</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>To highlight the potential applicability of the modified separator, full cells consisting of 100&nbsp;µm Na-metal anode, a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP)@C cathode active materials (mass loading: 5&nbsp;mg&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup>) and the AF5-coated separator were tested (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0005">5a</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23a–f</a>, Supporting Information). Specifically, the long-term durability of Na-metal cells with the AF5@GF separator under 1C has been measured. Evidently, an outstanding cycling stability and discharge capacity retention rate of 87.13% for 1000 cycles is achieved. This performance is far better than that of Na-metal full cells assembled with the GF, AF1@GF, and AF10@GF separators, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0005">5b</a>). Moreover, the voltage profiles obtained from cycling the different cells illustrate a single flat charge voltage plateau and two distinct discharge voltage plateaus during the initial cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0005">5c,d</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23g,h</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0052_R_d196546101e1293" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">52</a>, <a href="#adma202304942-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0053_R_d196546101e1293" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Ionics">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The charge voltage plateau is attributed to the extraction of Na ions from the NVP cathode, while the two discharge voltage plateaus are attributed to a two-step Na ion diffusion process resulting from slow Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202304942-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0052_R_d196546101e1300" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">52</a>-<a href="#adma202304942-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202304942-bib-0054_R_d196546101e1300" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Mater. Chem. A">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After that, the voltage hysteresis between these two discharge plateaus gradually decreases and these two platforms correspondingly become one upon subsequent cycling. In addition, the voltage hysteresis of the cell assembled with the AF5@GF separator slightly increases to 0.588&nbsp;V (1000 cycles), while the voltage hysteresis of the cell assembled with the bare GF separator enhances sharply to 0.846&nbsp;V after just 200 cycles.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202304942-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/76828b63-c056-46f9-923c-0999e6b1ab8a/adma202304942-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/76828b63-c056-46f9-923c-0999e6b1ab8a/adma202304942-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e6e25477-1db9-418a-81f2-07e2ed5e8d96/adma202304942-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/76828b63-c056-46f9-923c-0999e6b1ab8a/adma202304942-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202304942-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202304942" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic diagram of a Na-metal full cell. b) Long-term cycling performance at a specific current of 1C. c,d) Discharge/charge voltage profiles of the full cells of the Na|AF5@GF|NVP (c) and Na|GF|NVP (d). e) In situ EIS results of the Na|AF5@GF|NVP. f) Rate capability of the various full cells.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Furthermore, the interface evolution of different full cells was investigated by in situ EIS under galvanostatic charge–discharge mode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0005">5e</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The Na|AF5@GF|NVP full cell shows a notable reduction in the SEI impedance (<i>R</i><sub>f</sub>) from the initial state to the beginning of discharge. Meanwhile, the final <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> is very close to the initial state <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> after discharge, implying good reversibility of the Na|AF5@GF|NVP full cell. Conversely, the Na|GF|NVP full cell exhibits a significant increment in R<sub>f</sub> values within a single cycle. Differing from that of the Na|AF5@GF|NVP full cell, the value of <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> of the Na|GF|NVP full cell at the initially discharged state shows typical irreversible dendrite growth and SEI deterioration. A similar trend has been established in the evolution of the charge transfer impedance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) during the whole cycle. Additionally, the full cell incorporating the AF5@GF separator exhibits superior rate capability compared to those other separators (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202304942-fig-0005">5f</a>). The full cell utilizing the AF5@GF separator achieves a discharge capacity of 96.15&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 5C, which is higher than that achieved by cells incorporating the GF, AF1@GF, and AF10@GF separators.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202304942-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202304942-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>We propose a simple strategy of the separator modified by amyloid fibril biomolecules to achieve homogeneous, compact and reversible sodium-metal deposition. It is confirmed that the amyloid fibril can homogenize electric field distribution and ion concentration, reducing the growth of non-densely packed surface crystals. Both experimental and theoretical results indicate that amino acids in the amyloid fibril are strongly sodiophilic, and form stable SEI films during cycling, providing uniform Na ion transport and dendrite-free Na deposition. As a consequence, the modified symmetric cells can be cycled stably for 1800&nbsp;h at 0.5&nbsp;mA&nbsp;cm<sup>−2</sup> under an ester-based electrolyte, overwhelming the majority of similar systems reported so far. Moreover, the Na/Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> full cell containing amyloid fibril can operate for 1000 cycles and maintain a capacity retention of 87.13%. The successful exploration to overcome dendrite-growth in sodium-metal batteries demonstrates that the biomolecular-modified separator conception can be promisingly extended to other metal batteries with high reversibility.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
